<article class="ltx_document ltx_authors_1line">
<h1 class="ltx_title ltx_title_document" data-translate-id="chunk-0">
<span class="ltx_text" id="id1.1" style="position:relative; bottom:-0.1pt;"><img alt="[Uncaptioned image]" class="ltx_graphics ltx_img_square" height="21" id="id1.1.g1" src="x1.png" width="21"></span> Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence</h1>
<div class="ltx_authors">
<span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">
<span class="ltx_personname">Kimi Team
</span></span>
</div>
<div class="ltx_abstract">
<h6 class="ltx_title ltx_title_abstract" data-translate-id="chunk-1">Abstract</h6>
<p class="ltx_p" id="id2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-2">We introduce Kimi K2.5, an open-source multimodal agentic model designed to advance general agentic intelligence.
K2.5 emphasizes the joint optimization of text and vision so that two modalities enhance each other. This includes a series of techniques such as joint text-vision pre-training, zero-vision SFT, and joint text-vision reinforcement learning.
Building on this multimodal foundation, K2.5 introduces Agent Swarm, a self-directed parallel agent orchestration framework that dynamically decomposes complex tasks into heterogeneous sub-problems and executes them concurrently.
Extensive evaluations show that Kimi K2.5 achieves state-of-the-art results across various domains including coding, vision, reasoning, and agentic tasks. Agent Swarm also reduces latency by up to <math alttext="4.5\times" class="ltx_math_unparsed" display="inline" id="id2.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mn>4.5</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4.5\times</annotation></semantics></math> over single-agent baselines. We release the post-trained Kimi K2.5 model checkpoint<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote1"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">1</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://huggingface.co/moonshotai/Kimi-K2.5" title="">https://huggingface.co/moonshotai/Kimi-K2.5</a></span></span></span> to facilitate future research and real-world applications of agentic intelligence.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S0.F1"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="236" id="S0.F1.g1" src="figures/k25-main-result.png" width="429">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-3"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 1: </span>Kimi K2.5 main results.</figcaption>
</figure>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S1">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-4">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span>Introduction</h2>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-5">Large Language Models (LLMs) are rapidly evolving toward agentic intelligence. Recent advances, such as GPT-5.2&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib227" title="Introducing gpt 5.2">41</a>]</cite>, Claude Opus 4.5&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib226" title="Claude opus 4.5 system card">4</a>]</cite>, Gemini 3 Pro&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib228" title="Gemini 3 pro">19</a>]</cite>, and Kimi K2-Thinking&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib222" title="Introducing kimi k2 thinking">1</a>]</cite>, demonstrate substantial progress in agentic capabilities, particularly in tool calling and reasoning. These models increasingly exhibit the ability to decompose complex problems into multi-step plans and to execute long sequences of interleaved reasoning and actions.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-6">In this report, we introduce the training methods and evaluation results of Kimi K2.5. Concretely, we improve the training of K2.5 over previous models in the following two key aspects.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p3.1" data-translate-id="chunk-7"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.p3.1.1">Joint Optimization of Text and Vision.</span> A key insight from the practice of K2.5 is that joint optimization of text and vision enhances both modalities and avoids the conflict. Specifically, we devise a set of techniques for this purpose. During pre-training, in contrast to conventional approaches that add visual tokens to a text backbone at a late stage&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib17" title="Qwen3-vl technical report">7</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib18" title="Seed1.5-vl technical report">20</a>]</cite>, we find early vision fusion with lower ratios tends to yield better results given the fixed total vision-text tokens. Therefore, K2.5 mixes text and vision tokens with a constant ratio throughout the entire training process.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p4.1" data-translate-id="chunk-8">Architecturally, Kimi K2.5 employs MoonViT-3D, a native-resolution vision encoder incorporating the NaViT packing strategy&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib22" title="Patch n’ pack: navit, a vision transformer for any aspect ratio and resolution">14</a>]</cite>, enabling variable-resolution image inputs. For video understanding, we introduce a lightweight 3D ViT compression mechanism: consecutive frames are grouped in fours, processed through the shared MoonViT encoder, and temporally averaged at the patch level. This design allows Kimi K2.5 to process videos up to 4 <math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p4.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> longer within the same context window while maintaining complete weight sharing between image and video encoders.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p5">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p5.1" data-translate-id="chunk-9">During post-training, we introduce zero-vision SFT—text-only SFT alone activates visual reasoning and tool use.
We find that adding human-designed visual trajectories at this stage hurts generalization.
In contrast, text-only SFT performs better—likely because joint pretraining already establishes strong vision-text alignment, enabling capabilities to generalize naturally across modalities.
We then apply joint RL on both text and vision tasks.
Crucially, we find visual RL enhances textual performance rather than degrading it, with improvements on MMLU-Pro and GPQA-Diamond. This bidirectional enhancement—text bootstraps vision, vision refines text—represents superior cross-modal alignment in joint training.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p6">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p6.1" data-translate-id="chunk-10"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.p6.1.1">Agent Swarm: Parallel Agent Orchestration.</span>
Most existing agentic models rely on sequential execution of tool calls. Even systems capable of hundreds of reasoning steps, such as Kimi K2-Thinking&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib222" title="Introducing kimi k2 thinking">1</a>]</cite>, suffer from linear scaling of inference time, leading to unacceptable latency and limiting task complexity. As agentic workloads grow in scope and heterogeneity—e.g., building a complex project that involves massive-scale research, design, and development—the sequential paradigm becomes increasingly inefficient.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p7">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p7.1" data-translate-id="chunk-11">To overcome the latency and scalability limits of sequential agent execution, Kimi K2.5 introduces <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p7.1.1">Agent Swarm</span>, a dynamic framework for parallel agent orchestration. We propose a Parallel-Agent Reinforcement Learning (PARL) paradigm that departs from traditional agentic RL&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib224" title="Kimi-researcher end-to-end rl training for emerging agentic capabilities">2</a>]</cite>. In addition to optimizing tool execution via verifiable rewards, the model is equipped with interfaces for sub-agent creation and task delegation. During training, sub-agents are frozen and their execution trajectories are excluded from the optimization objective; only the orchestrator is updated via reinforcement learning. This decoupling circumvents two challenges of end-to-end co-optimization: credit assignment ambiguity and training instability. Agent Swarm enables complex tasks to be decomposed into heterogeneous sub-problems executed concurrently by domain-specialized agents, transforming task complexity from linear scaling to parallel processing. In wide-search scenarios, Agent Swarm reduces inference latency by up to 4.5<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p7.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> while improving item-level F1 from 72.8% to 79.0% compared to single-agent baselines.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S1.p8">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p8.1" data-translate-id="chunk-12">Kimi K2.5 represents a unified architecture for general-purpose agentic intelligence, integrating vision and language, thinking and instant modes, chats and agents. It achieves strong performance across a broad range of agentic and frontier benchmarks, including state-of-the-art results in visual-to-code generation (image/video-to-code) and real-world software engineering in our internal evaluations, while scaling both the diversity of specialized agents and the degree of parallelism. To accelerate community progress toward General Agentic Intelligence, we open-source our post-trained checkpoints of Kimi K2.5, enabling researchers and developers to explore, refine, and deploy scalable agentic intelligence.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S2">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-13">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span>Joint Optimization of Text and Vision</h2>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-14">Kimi K2.5 is a native multimodal model built upon Kimi K2 through large-scale joint pre-training on approximately 15 trillion mixed visual and text tokens. Unlike vision-adapted models that compromise either linguistic or visual capabilities, our joint pre-training paradigm enhances both modalities simultaneously. This section describes the multimodal joint optimization methodology that extends Kimi K2 to Kimi K2.5.</p>
</div>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS1">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-15">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.1 </span>Native Multimodal Pre-Training</h3>

<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-27">A key design question for multimodal pre-training is: Given a fixed vision-text token budget, what is the optimal vision-text joint-training strategy. Conventional wisdom&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib17" title="Qwen3-vl technical report">7</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib18" title="Seed1.5-vl technical report">20</a>]</cite> suggests introducing vision tokens predominantly in the later stages of LLM training at high ratios (e.g., 50% or higher) should accelerate multimodal capability acquisition, treating multimodal capability as a post-hoc add-on to linguistic competence.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-28">However, our experiments (as shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S2.T1" title="Table 1 ‣ 2.1 Native Multimodal Pre-Training ‣ 2 Joint Optimization of Text and Vision ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a> Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#A2.F9" title="Figure 9 ‣ Appendix B Pre-training ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">9</span></a>) reveal a different story. We conducted ablation studies varying the vision ratio and vision injection timing while keeping the total vision and text token budgets fixed. To strictly meet the targets for different ratios, we pre-trained the model with text-only tokens for a specifically calculated number of tokens before introducing vision data. Surprisingly, we found that the vision ratio has minimal impact on final multimodal performance. In fact, <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.1">early fusion with a lower vision ratio yields better results given a fixed total vision-text token budget</span>.
This motivates our native multimodal pre-training strategy: rather than aggressive vision-heavy training concentrated at the end, we adopt a moderate vision ratio integrated early in the training process, allowing the model to naturally develop balanced multimodal representations while benefiting from extended co-optimization of both modalities.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS2">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-29">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.2 </span>Zero-Vision SFT</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-30">Pretrained VLMs do not naturally perform vision-based tool-calling, which poses a cold-start problem for multimodal RL. Conventional approaches address this issue through manually annotated or prompt-engineered chain-of-thought (CoT) data&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib17" title="Qwen3-vl technical report">7</a>]</cite>, but such methods are limited in diversity, often restricting visual reasoning to simple diagrams and primitive tool manipulations (<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.1">crop</span>, <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.2">rotate</span>, <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.3">flip</span>).</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS2.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-31">An observation is that high-quality text SFT data are relatively abundant and diverse.
We propose a novel approach, zero-vision SFT, that uses only text SFT data to activate the visual, agentic capabilities during post-training.
In this approach, all image manipulations are proxied through programmatic operations in <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.1">IPython</span>, effectively serving as a generalization of traditional vision tool-use. This "zero-vision" activation enables diverse reasoning behaviors, including pixel-level operations such as object size estimation via binarization and counting, and generalizes to visually grounded tasks such as object localization, counting, and OCR.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS2.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p3.1" data-translate-id="chunk-32">Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Joint Multimodal Reinforcement Learning (RL) ‣ 2 Joint Optimization of Text and Vision ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a> illustrates the RL training curves, where the starting points are obtained from zero-vision SFT. The results show that zero-vision SFT is sufficient for activating vision capabilities while ensuring generalization across modalities. This phenomenon is likely due to the joint pretraining of text and vision data as described in Section&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S2.SS1" title="2.1 Native Multimodal Pre-Training ‣ 2 Joint Optimization of Text and Vision ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2.1</span></a>. Compared to zero-vision SFT, our preliminary experiments show that text-vision SFT yields much worse performance on visual, agentic tasks, possibly because of the lack of high-quality vision data.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS3">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-33">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.3 </span>Joint Multimodal Reinforcement Learning (RL)</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-34">In this section, we describe the methodology implemented in K2.5 that enables effective multimodal RL, from outcome-based visual RL to emergent cross-modal transfer that enhances textual performance.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S2.F2"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="353" id="S2.F2.g1" src="x2.png" width="595">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-35"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 2: </span>Vision RL training curves on vision benchmarks starting from minimal zero-vision SFT. By scaling vision RL FLOPs, the performance continues to improve, demonstrating that zero-vision activation paired with long-running RL is sufficient for acquiring robust visual capabilities.</figcaption>
</figure>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-36">Outcome-Based Visual RL</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-37">Following the zero-vision SFT, the model requires further refinement to reliably incorporate visual inputs into reasoning. Text-initiated activation alone exhibits notable failure modes: visual inputs are sometimes ignored, and images may not be attended to when necessary. We employ outcome-based RL on tasks that explicitly require visual comprehension for correct solutions.
We categorize these tasks into three domains:</p>
<ul class="ltx_itemize" id="S2.I1">
<li class="ltx_item" id="S2.I1.i1" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.I1.i1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.I1.i1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-38"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Visual grounding and counting:</span> Accurate localization and enumeration of objects within images;</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S2.I1.i2" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.I1.i2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.I1.i2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-39"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Chart and document understanding:</span> Interpretation of structured visual information and text extraction;</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S2.I1.i3" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.I1.i3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.I1.i3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-40"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Vision-critical STEM problems:</span> Mathematical and scientific questions filtered to require visual inputs.</p>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.2" data-translate-id="chunk-41">Outcome-based RL on these tasks improves both basic visual capabilities and more complex agentic behaviors. Extracting these trajectories for rejection-sampling fine-tuning (RFT) enables a self-improving data pipeline, allowing subsequent joint RL stages to leverage richer multimodal reasoning traces.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-42">Visual RL Improves Text Performance</h5>
<figure class="ltx_table" id="S2.T2">

<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.3" data-translate-id="chunk-44">To investigate potential trade-offs between visual and textual performance, we evaluated text-only benchmarks before and after visual RL. Surprisingly, outcome-based visual RL produced measurable improvements in textual tasks, including MMLU-Pro (84.7% <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\rightarrow</annotation></semantics></math> 86.4%), GPQA-Diamond (84.3% <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\rightarrow</annotation></semantics></math> 86.4%), and LongBench v2 (56.7% <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.3.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\rightarrow</annotation></semantics></math> 58.9%) (Table&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S2.T2" title="Table 2 ‣ Visual RL Improves Text Performance ‣ 2.3 Joint Multimodal Reinforcement Learning (RL) ‣ 2 Joint Optimization of Text and Vision ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>).
Analysis suggests that visual RL enhances calibration in areas requiring structured information extraction, reducing uncertainty on queries that resemble visually grounded reasoning (e.g., counting, OCR). These findings indicate that visual RL can contribute to cross-modal generalization, improving textual reasoning without observable degradation of language capabilities.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-45"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p2.1.1">Joint Multimodal RL</span> Motivated by the finding that robust visual capabilities can emerge from zero-vision SFT paired with vision RL—which further enhances general text abilities—we adopt a joint multimodal RL paradigm during Kimi K2.5’s post-training. Departing from conventional modality-specific expert divisions, we organize RL domains not by input modality but by abilities—knowledge, reasoning, coding, agentic, etc. These domain experts jointly learn from both pure-text and multimodal queries, while the Generative Reward Model (GRM) similarly optimizes across heterogeneous traces without modality barriers. This pardaigm ensures that capability improvements acquired through either textual or visual inputs inherently generalize to enhance related abilities across the alternate modality, thereby maximizing cross-modal capability transfer.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S3">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-46">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span>Agent Swarm</h2>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-47">The primary challenge of existing agent-based systems lies in their reliance on sequential execution of reasoning and tool-calling steps. While this structure may be effective for simpler, short-horizon tasks, it becomes inadequate as the complexity of the task increases and the accumulated context grows. As tasks evolve to contain broad information gathering and intricate, multi-branch reasoning, sequential systems often encounter significant bottlenecks <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib223" title="Building multi-agent systems: when and how to use them">6</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib226" title="Claude opus 4.5 system card">4</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib225" title="How we built our multi-agent research system">5</a>]</cite>. The limited capacity of a single agent working through each step one by one can lead to the exhaustion of practical reasoning depth and tool-call budgets, ultimately hindering the system’s ability to handle more complex scenarios.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-48">To address this, we introduce <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.p2.1.1">Agent Swarm</span> and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.p2.1.2">Parallel Agent Reinforcement Learning (PARL)</span>. Instead of executing a task as a reasoning chain or relying on pre-specified parallelization heuristics, K2.5 initiates an Agent Swarm through dynamic task decomposition, subagent instantiation, and parallel subtask scheduling. Importantly, parallelism is not presumed to be inherently advantageous; decisions regarding whether, when, and how to parallelize are explicitly learned through environmental feedback and RL-driven exploration.
As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S3.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ Prompt Construction for Parallel-agent Capability Induction ‣ 3 Agent Swarm ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>, the progression of performance demonstrates this adaptive capability, with the cumulative reward increasing smoothly as the orchestrator optimizes its parallelization strategy throughout training.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S3.F3"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="292" id="S3.F3.g1" src="figures/multi-agent-rl-system.png" width="539">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-49"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 3: </span>An agent swarm has a trainable orchestrator that dynamically creates specialized frozen subagents and decomposes complex tasks into parallelizable subtasks for efficient distributed execution.</figcaption>
</figure>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-50">Architecture and Learning Setup</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-51">The PARL framework adopts a decoupled architecture comprising a trainable orchestrator and frozen subagents instantiated from fixed intermediate policy checkpoints. This design deliberately avoids end-to-end co-optimization to circumvent two fundamental challenges: credit assignment ambiguity and training instability.
In this multi-agent setting, outcome-based rewards are inherently sparse and noisy; a correct final answer does not guarantee flawless subagent execution, just as a failure does not imply universal subagent error.
By freezing the subagents and treating their outputs as environmental observations rather than differentiable decision points, we disentangle high-level coordination logic from low-level execution proficiency,
leading to more robust convergence.
To improve efficiency, we first train the orchestrator using small-size subagents before transitioning to larger models.
Our RL framework also supports dynamically adjusting the inference instance ratios between subagents and the orchestrator, thereby maximizing the resource usage across the cluster.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-52">PARL Reward</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-53">Training a reliable parallel orchestrator is challenging due to the delayed, sparse, and non-stationary feedback inherent in independent subagent execution. To address this, we define the PARL reward as:</p>
<table class="ltx_equationgroup ltx_eqn_align ltx_eqn_table" id="A6.EGx1">
<tbody id="S3.Ex1"><tr class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_row ltx_align_baseline">
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padleft"></td>
<td class="ltx_td ltx_align_right ltx_eqn_cell"><math alttext="\displaystyle r_{\mathrm{PARL}}(x,y)=\lambda_{1}\cdot\mspace{-26.0mu}\underbrace{r_{\text{parallel}}}_{\text{instantiation reward}}\mspace{-9.0mu}+\mspace{18.0mu}\lambda_{2}\cdot\mspace{-32.0mu}\underbrace{r_{\text{finish}}}_{\text{sub-agent finish rate}}+\underbrace{r_{\text{perf}}(x,y)}_{\text{task-level outcome}}\,." class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.Ex1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>r</mi><mi>PARL</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>λ</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mpadded style="width:0em;" width="0em"><mo lspace="0.222em">⋅</mo></mpadded><munder><munder accentunder="true"><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>parallel</mtext></msub><mo stretchy="true">⏟</mo></munder><mtext>instantiation reward</mtext></munder></mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow><mspace style="width:1em;" width="1em"></mspace><mrow><mrow><msub><mi>λ</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mpadded style="width:0em;" width="0em"><mo lspace="0.222em">⋅</mo></mpadded><munder><munder accentunder="true"><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>finish</mtext></msub><mo stretchy="true">⏟</mo></munder><mtext>sub-agent finish rate</mtext></munder></mrow><mo>+</mo><munder><munder accentunder="true"><mrow><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>perf</mtext></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="true">⏟</mo></munder><mtext>task-level outcome</mtext></munder></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle r_{\mathrm{PARL}}(x,y)=\lambda_{1}\cdot\mspace{-26.0mu}\underbrace{r_{\text{parallel}}}_{\text{instantiation reward}}\mspace{-9.0mu}+\mspace{18.0mu}\lambda_{2}\cdot\mspace{-32.0mu}\underbrace{r_{\text{finish}}}_{\text{sub-agent finish rate}}+\underbrace{r_{\text{perf}}(x,y)}_{\text{task-level outcome}}\,.</annotation></semantics></math></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padright"></td>
</tr></tbody>
</table>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.6" data-translate-id="chunk-54">The performance reward <math alttext="r_{\text{perf}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>perf</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">r_{\text{perf}}</annotation></semantics></math> evaluates the overall success and quality of the solution <math alttext="y" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>y</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">y</annotation></semantics></math> for a given task <math alttext="x" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.3.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>x</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">x</annotation></semantics></math>. This is augmented by two auxiliary rewards, each addressing a distinct challenge in learning parallel orchestration. The reward <math alttext="r_{\text{parallel}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.4.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>parallel</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">r_{\text{parallel}}</annotation></semantics></math> is introduced to mitigate <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.6.1">serial collapse</em>—a local optimum where the orchestrator defaults to single-agent execution. By incentivizing subagent instantiation, this term encourages the exploration of concurrent scheduling spaces. The <math alttext="r_{\text{finish}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.5.m5" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>finish</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">r_{\text{finish}}</annotation></semantics></math> reward focuses on the successful completion of assigned subtasks. It is used to prevent <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.6.2">spurious parallelism</em>, a reward-hacking behavior in which the orchestrator increases parallel metrics dramatically by spawning many subagents without meaningful task decomposition. By rewarding completed subtasks, <math alttext="r_{\text{finish}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p2.6.m6" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>r</mi><mtext>finish</mtext></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">r_{\text{finish}}</annotation></semantics></math> enforces feasibility and guides the policy toward valid and effective decompositions.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p3.2" data-translate-id="chunk-55">To ensure the final policy optimizes for the primary objective, the hyperparameters <math alttext="\lambda_{1}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p3.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>λ</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\lambda_{1}</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="\lambda_{2}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p3.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>λ</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\lambda_{2}</annotation></semantics></math> are annealed to zero over the course of training.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-56">Critical Steps as Resource Constraint</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.7" data-translate-id="chunk-57">To measure computational time cost in a parallel-agent setting, we define <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.7.1">critical steps</em> by analogy to the <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.7.2">critical path</em> in a computation graph.
We model an episode as a sequence of execution stages indexed by <math alttext="t=1,\dots,T" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mi>t</mi><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">…</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>T</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">t=1,\dots,T</annotation></semantics></math>.
In each stage, the main agent executes an action, which corresponds to either direct tool invocation or the instantiation of a group of subagents running in parallel. Let <math alttext="S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><msubsup><mi>S</mi><mi>main</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></msubsup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}</annotation></semantics></math> denote the number of steps taken by the main agent in stage <math alttext="t" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.3.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>t</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">t</annotation></semantics></math> (typically <math alttext="S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}=1" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.4.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><msubsup><mi>S</mi><mi>main</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></msubsup><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}=1</annotation></semantics></math>), and <math alttext="S_{\mathrm{sub},i}^{(t)}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.5.m5" intent=":literal"><semantics><msubsup><mi>S</mi><mrow><mi>sub</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>i</mi></mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></msubsup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">S_{\mathrm{sub},i}^{(t)}</annotation></semantics></math> denote the number of steps taken by the <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.6.m6" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>i</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">i</annotation></semantics></math>-th subagent in that parallel group.
The duration of stage <math alttext="t" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.7.m7" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>t</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">t</annotation></semantics></math> is governed by the longest-running subagent within that cohort. Consequently, the total critical steps for an episode are defined as</p>
<table class="ltx_equationgroup ltx_eqn_align ltx_eqn_table" id="A6.EGx2">
<tbody id="S3.Ex2"><tr class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_row ltx_align_baseline">
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padleft"></td>
<td class="ltx_td ltx_align_right ltx_eqn_cell"><math alttext="\displaystyle\text{CriticalSteps}=\sum_{t=1}^{T}\left(S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}+\max_{i}S_{\mathrm{sub},i}^{(t)}\right)." class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.Ex2.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mtext>CriticalSteps</mtext><mo>=</mo><mrow><mstyle displaystyle="true"><munderover><mo movablelimits="false">∑</mo><mrow><mi>t</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>T</mi></munderover></mstyle><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><msubsup><mi>S</mi><mi>main</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></msubsup><mo>+</mo><mrow><munder><mi>max</mi><mi>i</mi></munder><mo lspace="0.167em">⁡</mo><msubsup><mi>S</mi><mrow><mi>sub</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>i</mi></mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></msubsup></mrow></mrow><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle\text{CriticalSteps}=\sum_{t=1}^{T}\left(S_{\mathrm{main}}^{(t)}+\max_{i}S_{\mathrm{sub},i}^{(t)}\right).</annotation></semantics></math></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padright"></td>
</tr></tbody>
</table>
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.8" data-translate-id="chunk-58">By constraining training and evaluation using critical steps rather than total steps, the framework explicitly incentivizes effective parallelization. Excessive subtask creation that does not reduce the maximum execution time of parallel groups yields little benefit under this metric, while well-balanced task decomposition that shortens the longest parallel branch directly reduces critical steps. As a result, the orchestrator is encouraged to allocate work across subagents in a way that minimizes end-to-end latency, rather than merely maximizing concurrency or total work performed.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px4">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-59">Prompt Construction for Parallel-agent Capability Induction</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-60">To incentivize the orchestrator to leverage the advantages of parallelization, we construct a suite of synthetic prompts designed to stress the limits of sequential agentic execution.
These prompts emphasize either <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1.1.1">wide search</em>, requiring simultaneous exploration of many independent information sources, or <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S3.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1.1.2">deep search</em>, requiring multiple reasoning branches with delayed aggregation. We additionally include tasks inspired by real-world workloads, such as long-context document analysis and large-scale file downloading.
When executed sequentially, these tasks are difficult to complete within fixed reasoning-step and tool-call budgets. By construction, they encourage the orchestrator to allocate subtasks in parallel, enabling completion within fewer critical steps than would be feasible for a single sequential agent. Importantly, the prompts do not explicitly instruct the model to parallelize. Instead, they shape the task distribution such that parallel decomposition and scheduling strategies are naturally favored.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S3.F4"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="243" id="S3.F4.g1" src="x3.png" width="830">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-61"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 4: </span>In our parallel-agent reinforcement learning environment, the training accuracy increases smoothly as training progresses. At the same time, the level of parallelism during training also gradually increases.</figcaption>
</figure>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S4">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-62">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">4 </span>Method Overview</h2>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S4.SS1">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-63">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.1 </span>Foundation: Kimi K2 Base Model</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-64">The foundation of Kimi K2.5 is Kimi K2&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite>, a trillion-parameter mixture-of-experts (MoE) transformer&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib67" title="Attention is all you need">59</a>]</cite> model pre-trained on 15 trillion high-quality text tokens. Kimi K2 employs the token-efficient MuonClip optimizer&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib180" title="Muon: an optimizer for hidden layers in neural networks">29</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib179" title="Muon is scalable for llm training">33</a>]</cite> with QK-Clip for training stability. The model comprises 1.04 trillion total parameters with 32 billion activated parameters, utilizing 384 experts with 8 activated per token (sparsity of 48). For detailed descriptions of MuonClip, architecture design, and training infrastructure, we refer to the Kimi K2 technical report&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite>.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S4.SS2">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-65">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.2 </span>Model Architecture</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-66">The multimodal architecture of Kimi K2.5 consists of three components: a three-dimensional native-resolution vision encoder (MoonViT-3D), an MLP projector, and the Kimi K2 MoE language model, following the design principles established in Kimi-VL&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib13" title="Kimi-vl technical report">54</a>]</cite>.</p>
</div>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-67">MoonViT-3D: Shared Embedding Space for Images and Videos</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-68">In Kimi-VL, we employ MoonViT to natively process images at their original resolutions, eliminating the need for complex sub-image splitting and splicing operations. Initialized from SigLIP-SO-400M&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib23" title="Sigmoid loss for language image pre-training">77</a>]</cite>, MoonViT incorporates the patch packing strategy from NaViT&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib22" title="Patch n’ pack: navit, a vision transformer for any aspect ratio and resolution">14</a>]</cite>, where single images are divided into patches, flattened, and sequentially concatenated into 1D sequences, thereby enabling efficient simultaneous training on images at varying resolutions.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-69">To maximize the transfer of image understanding capabilities to video, we introduce <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p2.1.1">MoonViT-3D</span> with a unified architecture, fully shared parameters, and a consistent embedding space. By generalizing the “patch n’ pack“ philosophy to the temporal dimension, up to four consecutive frames are treated as a spatiotemporal volume: 2D patches from these frames are jointly flattened and packed into a single 1D sequence, allowing the identical attention mechanism to operate seamlessly across both space and time. While the extra temporal attention improves understanding on high-speed motions and visual effects, the sharing maximizes knowledge generalization from static images to dynamic videos, achieving strong video understanding performance (see in Tab.&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.T4" title="Table 4 ‣ Benchmarks ‣ 5.1.1 Evaluation Settings ‣ 5.1 Main Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>) without requiring specialized video modules or architectural bifurcation. Prior to the MLP projector, lightweight temporal pooling aggregates patches within each temporal chunk, yielding <math alttext="4\times" class="ltx_math_unparsed" display="inline" id="S4.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p2.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mn>4</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4\times</annotation></semantics></math> temporal compression to significantly extend feasible video length. The result is a unified pipeline where knowledge and ability obtained from image pretraining transfers holistically to videos through one shared parameter space and feature representation.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S4.SS3">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-70">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.3 </span>Pre-training Pipeline</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-71">As illustrated in Table&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S4.T3" title="Table 3 ‣ 4.3 Pre-training Pipeline ‣ 4 Method Overview ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>, Kimi K2.5’s pre-training builds upon the Kimi K2 language model checkpoint and processes approximately 15T tokens across three stages: first, standalone ViT training to establish a robust native-resolution visual encoder; second, joint pre-training to simultaneously enhance language and multimodal capabilities; and third, mid-training on high-quality data and long-context activation to refine capabilities and extend context windows.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_table" id="S4.T3">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-72"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 3: </span>Overview of training stages: data composition, token volumes, sequence lengths, and trainable components.</figcaption>

<td class="ltx_td ltx_align_center ltx_border_bb ltx_border_t" id="S4.T3.2.5.4" style="padding-top:1pt;padding-bottom:1pt;">ViT &amp; LLM</td>

<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-73">ViT Training Stage</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-74">The MoonViT-3D is continual pre-trained from SigLIP&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib23" title="Sigmoid loss for language image pre-training">77</a>]</cite> on image-text and video-text pairs, where the text components consist of a variety of targets: image alt texts, synthetic captions of images and videos, grounding bboxes, and OCR texts. Unlike the implementation in Kimi-VL&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib13" title="Kimi-vl technical report">54</a>]</cite>, this continual pre-training does not include a contrastive loss, but incorporates solely cross-entropy loss <math alttext="{L}_{caption}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>L</mi><mrow><mi>c</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>a</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>p</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>t</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>i</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>o</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>n</mi></mrow></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{L}_{caption}</annotation></semantics></math> for caption generation conditioned on input images and videos. We adopt a two-stage alignment strategy. In the first stage, we update the MoonViT-3D to align it with Moonlight-16B-A3B&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib179" title="Muon is scalable for llm training">33</a>]</cite> via the caption loss, consuming about 1T tokens with very few training FLOPs. This stage allows MoonViT-3D to primarily understand high-resolution images and videos. A very short second stage follows, updating only the MLP projector to bridge the ViT with the 1T LLM for smoother joint pre-training.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-75">Joint Training Stages</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS3.SSS0.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-76">The joint pre-training stage continues from a near-end Kimi K2 checkpoint over additional 15T vision-text tokens at 4K sequence length. The data recipe extends Kimi K2’s pre-training distribution by introducing unique tokens, adjusting data proportions with increased weight on coding-related content, and controlling maximum epochs per data source. The third stage performs long-context activation with integrated higher-quality mid-training data, sequentially extending context length via YaRN&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib45" title="Yarn: efficient context window extension of large language models">44</a>]</cite> interpolation. This yields significant generalization improvements in long-context text understanding and long video comprehension.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S4.SS4">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-77">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.4 </span>Post-Training</h3>
<section class="ltx_subsubsection" id="S4.SS4.SSS1">
<h4 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsubsection" data-translate-id="chunk-78">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">4.4.1 </span>Supervised Fine-Tuning</h4>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-79">Following the SFT pipeline established by Kimi K2 <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite>, we developed K2.5 by synthesizing high-quality candidate responses from K2, K2 Thinking and a suite of proprietary in-house expert models. Our data generation strategy employs specialized pipelines tailored to specific domains, integrating human annotation with advanced prompt engineering and multi-stage verification.
This methodology produced a large-scale instruction-tuning dataset featuring diverse prompts and intricate reasoning trajectories, ultimately training the model to prioritize interactive reasoning and precise tool-calling for complex, real-world applications.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsubsection" id="S4.SS4.SSS2">
<h4 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsubsection" data-translate-id="chunk-80">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">4.4.2 </span>Reinforcement Learning</h4>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-81">Reinforcement learning constitutes a crucial phase of our post-training.
To facilitate joint optimization across text and vision modalities, as well as to enable PARL for agent swarm, we develop a Unified Agentic Reinforcement Learning Environment (Appendix&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#A4" title="Appendix D Unified Agentic Reinforcement Learning Environment ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">D</span></a>) and optimize the RL algorithms.
Both text-vision joint RL and PARL are built upon the algorithms described in this section.</p>
</div>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-82">Policy Optimization</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.6" data-translate-id="chunk-83">For each problem <math alttext="x" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>x</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">x</annotation></semantics></math> sampled from a dataset <math alttext="\mathcal{D}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic">𝒟</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathcal{D}</annotation></semantics></math>, <math alttext="K" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.3.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>K</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">K</annotation></semantics></math> responses <math alttext="\{y_{1},\dots,y_{K}\}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.4.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mo>,</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">…</mi><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>K</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\{y_{1},\dots,y_{K}\}</annotation></semantics></math> are generated using the previous policy <math alttext="\pi_{\mathrm{old}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.5.m5" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>π</mi><mi>old</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\pi_{\mathrm{old}}</annotation></semantics></math>.
We optimize the model <math alttext="\pi_{\theta}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.6.m6" intent=":literal"><semantics><msub><mi>π</mi><mi>θ</mi></msub><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\pi_{\theta}</annotation></semantics></math> with respect to the following objective:</p>
<table class="ltx_equationgroup ltx_eqn_align ltx_eqn_table" id="A6.EGx3">
<tbody id="S4.E1"><tr class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_row ltx_align_baseline">
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padleft"></td>
<td class="ltx_td ltx_align_right ltx_eqn_cell"><math alttext="\displaystyle L_{\mathrm{RL}}(\theta)=\mathbb{E}_{x\sim\mathcal{D}}\left[\frac{1}{N}\sum_{j=1}^{K}\sum_{i=1}^{|y_{j}|}\mathrm{Clip}\left(\frac{\pi_{\theta}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}{\pi_{\mathrm{old}}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})},\alpha,\beta\right)({r}(x,y_{j})-\bar{r}(x))-\tau\left(\log\frac{\pi_{\theta}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}{\pi_{\mathrm{old}}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}\right)^{2}\right]\,." stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mover accent="true"><mi>r</mi><mo>¯</mo></mover><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo>−</mo><mrow><mi>τ</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><msup><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mi>log</mi><mo lspace="0.167em">⁡</mo><mstyle displaystyle="true"><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>π</mi><mi>θ</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><msub><mi>π</mi><mi>old</mi></msub><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><msubsup><mi>y</mi><mi>j</mi><mi>i</mi></msubsup><mo fence="false">|</mo><mrow><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><msubsup><mi>y</mi><mi>j</mi><mrow><mn>0</mn><mo lspace="0.278em"
