encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle L_{\mathrm{RL}}(\theta)=\mathbb{E}_{x\sim\mathcal{D}}\left[\frac{1}{N}\sum_{j=1}^{K}\sum_{i=1}^{|y_{j}|}\mathrm{Clip}\left(\frac{\pi_{\theta}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}{\pi_{\mathrm{old}}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})},\alpha,\beta\right)({r}(x,y_{j})-\bar{r}(x))-\tau\left(\log\frac{\pi_{\theta}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}{\pi_{\mathrm{old}}(y_{j}^{i}|x,y_{j}^{0:i})}\right)^{2}\right]\,.</annotation></semantics></math></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padright"></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_eqno ltx_align_middle ltx_align_right" rowspan="1"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(1)</span></td>
</tr></tbody>
</table>
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.12" data-translate-id="chunk-84">Here <math alttext="\alpha,\beta,\tau>0" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.7.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi>α</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>β</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>τ</mi></mrow><mo>&gt;</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha,\beta,\tau&gt;0</annotation></semantics></math> are hyperparameters,
<math alttext="y^{j}_{0:i}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.8.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><msubsup><mi>y</mi><mrow><mn>0</mn><mo lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em">:</mo><mi>i</mi></mrow><mi>j</mi></msubsup><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">y^{j}_{0:i}</annotation></semantics></math> is the prefix up to the <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.9.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>i</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">i</annotation></semantics></math>-th token of the <math alttext="j" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.10.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>j</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">j</annotation></semantics></math>-th response,
<math alttext="N=\sum_{i=1}^{K}|y_{i}|" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.11.m5" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mi>N</mi><mo rspace="0.111em">=</mo><mrow><msubsup><mo rspace="0em">∑</mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>K</mi></msubsup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">|</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">|</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">N=\sum_{i=1}^{K}|y_{i}|</annotation></semantics></math> is the total number of generated tokens in a batch,
<math alttext="\bar{r}(x)=\frac{1}{K}\sum_{j=1}^{K}r(x,y_{j})" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p1.12.m6" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mover accent="true"><mi>r</mi><mo>¯</mo></mover><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mi>K</mi></mfrac><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><msubsup><mo>∑</mo><mrow><mi>j</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>K</mi></msubsup><mrow><mi>r</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\bar{r}(x)=\frac{1}{K}\sum_{j=1}^{K}r(x,y_{j})</annotation></semantics></math> is the mean reward of all generated responses.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-85">This loss function departs from the policy optimization algorithm used in K1.5&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib107" title="Kimi k1. 5: scaling reinforcement learning with llms">30</a>]</cite> by introducing a token-level clipping mechanism designed to mitigate the off-policy divergence amplified by discrepancies between training and inference frameworks.
The mechanism functions as a simple gradient masking scheme: policy gradients are computed normally for tokens with log-ratios within the interval <math alttext="[\alpha,\beta]" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px1.p2.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">[</mo><mi>α</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>β</mi><mo stretchy="false">]</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">[\alpha,\beta]</annotation></semantics></math>, while gradients for tokens falling outside this range are zeroed out.
Notably, a key distinction from standard PPO clipping <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib203" title="Proximal policy optimization algorithms">50</a>]</cite> is that our method relies strictly on the log-ratio to explicitly bound off-policy drift, regardless of the sign of the advantages.
This approach aligns with recent strategies proposed to stabilize large-scale RL training <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib201" title="Your efficient rl framework secretly brings you off-policy rl training">74</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib202" title="Small leak can sink a great ship–boost rl training on moe with icepop!">78</a>]</cite>.
Empirically, we find this mechanism essential for maintaining training stability in complex domains requiring long-horizon, multi-step tool-use reasoning.
We employ the MuonClip optimizer&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib180" title="Muon: an optimizer for hidden layers in neural networks">29</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib179" title="Muon is scalable for llm training">33</a>]</cite> to minimize this objective.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-86">Reward Function</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-87">We apply a rule-based outcome reward for tasks with verifiable solutions, such as reasoning and agentic tasks.
To optimize resource consumption, we also incorporate a budget-control reward aimed at enhancing token efficiency. For general-purpose tasks, we employ Generative Reward Models (GRMs) that provide granular evaluations aligned with Kimi’s internal value criteria.
In addition, for visual tasks, we design task-specific reward functions to provide fine-grained supervision.
For visual grounding and point localization tasks, we employ an F1-based reward with soft matching: grounding tasks derive soft matches from Intersection over Union (IoU) and point tasks derive soft matches from Gaussian-weighted distances under optimal matching.
For polygon segmentation tasks, we rasterize the predicted polygon into a binary mask and compute the segmentation IoU against the ground-truth mask to assign the reward.
For OCR tasks, we adopt normalized edit distance to quantify character-level alignment between predictions and ground-truth.
For counting tasks, rewards are assigned based on the absolute difference between predictions and ground-truth.
Furthermore, we synthesize complex visual puzzle problems and utilize an LLM verifier (Kimi K2) to provide feedback.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px3">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-88">Generative Reward Models</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-89">Kimi K2 leverages a self-critique rubric reward for open-ended generation <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite>, and K2.5 extends this line of work by systematically deploying <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px3.p1.1.1">Generative Reward Models (GRMs)</em> across a broad range of agentic behaviors and multimodal trajectories. Rather than limiting reward modeling to conversational outputs, we apply GRMs on top of verified reward signals in diverse environments, including chat assistants, coding agents, search agents, and artifact-generating agents. Notably, GRMs function not as binary adjudicators, but as fine-grained evaluators aligned with Kimi’s values that are critical to user experiences, such as helpfulness, response readiness, contextual relevance, appropriate level of detail, aesthetic quality of generated artifacts, and strict instruction following. This design allows the reward signal to capture nuanced preference gradients that are difficult to encode with purely rule-based or task-specific verifiers.
To mitigate reward hacking and overfitting to a single preference signal, we employ multiple alternative GRM rubrics tailored to different task contexts.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-90">Token Efficient Reinforcement Learning</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-91">Token efficiency is central to LLMs with test-time scaling.
While test-time scaling inherently trades computation for reasoning quality, practical gains require algorithmic innovations that actively navigate this trade-off.
Our previous findings indicate that imposing a problem-dependent budget effectively constrains inference-time compute, incentivizing the model to generate more concise chain of thought reasoning patterns without unnecessary token expansion <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib107" title="Kimi k1. 5: scaling reinforcement learning with llms">30</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite>.
However, we also observe a <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p1.1.1">length-overfitting phenomenon</em>: models trained under rigid budget constraints often fail to generalize to higher compute scales. Consequently, they cannot effectively leverage additional inference-time tokens to solve complex problems, instead defaulting to truncated reasoning patterns.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-92">To this end, we propose <em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.1.1">Toggle</em>,
a training heuristic that alternates between inference-time scaling and budget-constrained optimization:
for learning iteration <math alttext="t" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>t</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">t</annotation></semantics></math>,
the reward function is defined by</p>
<table class="ltx_equationgroup ltx_eqn_align ltx_eqn_table" id="A6.EGx4">
<tbody id="S4.Ex3"><tr class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_row ltx_align_baseline">
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padleft"></td>
<td class="ltx_td ltx_align_right ltx_eqn_cell"><math alttext="\displaystyle\tilde{r}(x,y)=\begin{cases}r(x,y)\cdot\mathbb{I}\left\{\frac{1}{K}\sum_{i=1}^{K}r(x,y_{i})<\lambda\ \mathrm{or}\ |y_{i}|\leq\mathrm{budget(x)}\right\}&amp;\text{if }\lfloor t/m\rfloor\pmod{2}=0\ (\mathrm{{Phase0}})\\
r(x,y)&amp;\text{if }\lfloor t/m\rfloor\pmod{2}=1\ (\mathrm{{Phase1}})\end{cases}\,." class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.Ex3.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mover accent="true"><mi>r</mi><mo>~</mo></mover><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mo>{</mo><mtable columnspacing="5pt" rowspacing="0pt"><mtr><mtd class="ltx_align_left" columnalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>r</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>y</mi><mo rspace="0.055em" stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo rspace="0.222em">⋅</mo><mi>𝕀</mi></mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>{</mo><mrow><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mi>K</mi></mfrac><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><msubsup><mo>∑</mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mi>K</mi></msubsup><mrow><mi>r</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo>&lt;</mo><mrow><mi>λ</mi><mo lspace="0.500em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mi>or</mi><mo lspace="0.500em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">|</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">|</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>≤</mo><mrow><mi>budget</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo>}</mo></mrow></mrow></mtd><mtd class="ltx_align_left" columnalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mrow><mtext>if&nbsp;</mtext><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⌊</mo><mrow><mi>t</mi><mo>/</mo><mi>m</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">⌋</mo></mrow></mrow><mspace style="width:0.949em;" width="0.949em"></mspace><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0.167em">mod</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>0</mn><mo lspace="0.500em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>Phase0</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd class="ltx_align_left" columnalign="left"><mrow><mi>r</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mtd><mtd class="ltx_align_left" columnalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mrow><mtext>if&nbsp;</mtext><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">⌊</mo><mrow><mi>t</mi><mo>/</mo><mi>m</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">⌋</mo></mrow></mrow><mspace style="width:0.949em;" width="0.949em"></mspace><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0.167em">mod</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo lspace="0.500em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>Phase1</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow></mtd></mtr></mtable></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\displaystyle\tilde{r}(x,y)=\begin{cases}r(x,y)\cdot\mathbb{I}\left\{\frac{1}{K}\sum_{i=1}^{K}r(x,y_{i})&lt;\lambda\ \mathrm{or}\ |y_{i}|\leq\mathrm{budget(x)}\right\}&amp;\text{if }\lfloor t/m\rfloor\pmod{2}=0\ (\mathrm{{Phase0}})\\
r(x,y)&amp;\text{if }\lfloor t/m\rfloor\pmod{2}=1\ (\mathrm{{Phase1}})\end{cases}\,.</annotation></semantics></math></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padright"></td>
</tr></tbody>
</table>
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.5" data-translate-id="chunk-93">where <math alttext="\lambda" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.2.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>λ</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\lambda</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="m" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.3.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>m</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">m</annotation></semantics></math> are hyper-parameters of the algorithm and <math alttext="K" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.4.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>K</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">K</annotation></semantics></math> is the number of rollouts per problem.
Specifically, the algorithm alternates between two optimization phases every <math alttext="m" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p2.5.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>m</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">m</annotation></semantics></math> iterations:</p>
<ul class="ltx_itemize" id="S4.I1">
<li class="ltx_item" id="S4.I1.i1" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.I1.i1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.I1.i1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-94">Phase0 (<em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I1.i1.p1.1.1">budget limited phase</em>):
The model is trained to solve the problem within a task-dependent token budget.
To prevent a premature sacrifice of quality for efficiency, this constraint is conditionally applied: it is only enforced when the model’s mean accuracy for a given problem exceeds the threshold <math alttext="\lambda" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.I1.i1.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>λ</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\lambda</annotation></semantics></math>.</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S4.I1.i2" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.I1.i2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.I1.i2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-95">Phase1 (<em class="ltx_emph ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I1.i2.p1.1.1">standard scaling phase</em>): The model generates responses up to the maximum token limit, encouraging the model to leverage computation for better inference-time scaling.</p>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p3.1" data-translate-id="chunk-96">The problem-dependent budget is estimated from the <math alttext="\rho" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p3.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mi>ρ</mi><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\rho</annotation></semantics></math>-th percentile of token lengths among the subset of correct responses:</p>
<table class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_table" id="S4.E2">
<tbody><tr class="ltx_equation ltx_eqn_row ltx_align_baseline">
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padleft"></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_align_center"><math alttext="\mathrm{budget}(x)=\text{Percentile}\left(\{|y_{j}|\mid r(x,y_{i})=1,i=1,\dots,K\},\rho\right)\,." class="ltx_Math" display="block" id="S4.E2.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>budget</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mtext>Percentile</mtext><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo>(</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">{</mo><mrow><mrow><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">|</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>j</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">|</mo></mrow><mo>∣</mo><mrow><mi>r</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">​</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>y</mi><mi>i</mi></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mo>,</mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">…</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>K</mi></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy="false">}</mo></mrow><mo>,</mo><mi>ρ</mi><mo>)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><mo lspace="0.170em">.</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{budget}(x)=\text{Percentile}\left(\{|y_{j}|\mid r(x,y_{i})=1,i=1,\dots,K\},\rho\right)\,.</annotation></semantics></math></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_center_padright"></td>
<td class="ltx_eqn_cell ltx_eqn_eqno ltx_align_middle ltx_align_right" rowspan="1"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(2)</span></td>
</tr></tbody>
</table>
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p3.2" data-translate-id="chunk-97">This budget is estimated once at the beginning of training and remains fixed thereafter.
Notably, Toggle functions as a stochastic alternating optimization for a bi-objective problem. It is specifically designed to reconcile reasoning capabilities with computational efficiency.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S4.F5"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="274" id="S4.F5.g1" src="figures/te-k2-thinking-radar.png" width="479">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-98"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 5: </span>Comparison of model performance and token usage for Kimi K2 Thinking following token-efficient RL.</figcaption>
</figure>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p4.1" data-translate-id="chunk-99">We evaluate the effectiveness of Toggle on K2 Thinking&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib222" title="Introducing kimi k2 thinking">1</a>]</cite>.
As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S4.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ Token Efficient Reinforcement Learning ‣ 4.4.2 Reinforcement Learning ‣ 4.4 Post-Training ‣ 4 Method Overview ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>, we observe a consistent reduction in output length across nearly all benchmarks.
On average, Toggle decreases output tokens by 25<math alttext="\sim" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.SS4.SSS2.Px4.p4.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>∼</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\sim</annotation></semantics></math>30% with a negligible impact on performance.
We also observe that redundant patterns in the chain-of-thought, such as repeated verifications and mechanical calculations, decrease substantially.
Furthermore, Toggle shows strong domain generalization.
For example, when trained exclusively on mathematics and programming tasks, the model still achieves consistent token reductions on GPQA and MMLU-Pro with only marginal degradation in performance (Figure <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S4.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ Token Efficient Reinforcement Learning ‣ 4.4.2 Reinforcement Learning ‣ 4.4 Post-Training ‣ 4 Method Overview ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>).</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S4.SS5">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-100">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.5 </span>Training Infrastructure</h3>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS5.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS5.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-101">Kimi K2.5 inherits the training infrastructure from Kimi K2&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib108" title="Kimi k2: open agentic intelligence">53</a>]</cite> with minimal modifications. For multimodal training, we propose Decoupled Encoder Process, where the vision encoder is incorporated into the existing pipeline with negligible additional overhead.</p>
</div>
<section class="ltx_subsubsection" id="S4.SS5.SSS1">
<h4 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsubsection" data-translate-id="chunk-102">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">4.5.1 </span>Decoupled Encoder Process (DEP)</h4>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-103">In a typical multimodal training paradigm utilizing Pipeline Parallelism (PP), the vision encoder and text embedding are co-located in the first stage of the pipeline (Stage-0). However, due to the inherent variations of multimodal input size (e.g., image counts and resolutions), Stage-0 suffers from drastic fluctuations in both computational load and memory usage. This forces existing solutions to adopt custom PP configurations for vision-language models — for instance, <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib13" title="Kimi-vl technical report">54</a>]</cite> manually adjusts the number of text decoder layers in Stage-0 to reserve memory. While this compromise alleviates memory pressure, it does not fundamentally resolve the load imbalance caused by multimodal input sizes. More critically, it precludes the direct reuse of parallel strategies that have been highly optimized for text-only training.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-104">Leveraging the unique topological position of the visual encoder within the computation graph — specifically, its role as the start of the forward pass and the end of the backward pass — our training uses <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p2.1.1">Decoupled Encoder Process (DEP)</span>, which is composed of three stages in each training step:</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p3">
<ul class="ltx_itemize" id="S4.I2">
<li class="ltx_item" id="S4.I2.i1" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.I2.i1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.I2.i1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-105"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Balanced Vision Forward:</span> We first execute the forward pass for all visual data in the global batch. Because the vision encoder is small, we replicate it on all GPUs regardless of other parallelism strategies. During this phase, the forward computational workload is evenly distributed across all GPUs based on load metrics (e.g., image or patch counts). This eliminates load-imbalance caused by PP and visual token counts. To minimize peak memory usage, we discard all intermediate activations, retaining only the final output activations. The results are gathered back to PP Stage-0;</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S4.I2.i2" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S4.I2.i2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.I2.i2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-106"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.p1.1.1">Backbone Training:</span> This phase performs the forward and backward passes for the main transformer backbone. By discarding intermediate activations in the preceding phase, we can now fully leverage any efficient parallel strategies validated in pure text training. After this phase, gradients are accumulated at the visual encoder output;</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S4.I2.i3" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S4.I2.i3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.I2.i3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-107"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i3.p1.1.1">Vision Recomputation &amp; Backward:</span> We re-compute the vision encoder forward pass, followed by a backward pass to compute gradients for parameters in the vision encoder;</p>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S4.SS5.SSS1.p4.1" data-translate-id="chunk-108">DEP not only achieves load-balance, but also decouples the optimization strategy of the vision encoder and the main backbone. K2.5 seamlessly inherits the parallel strategy of K2, achieving a multimodal training efficiency of 90% relative to text-only training. We note a concurrent work, LongCat-Flash-Omni&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib204" title="Longcat-flash-omni technical report">55</a>]</cite>, shares a similar design philosophy.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S5">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-109">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">5 </span>Evaluations</h2>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S5.SS1">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-110">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.1 </span>Main Results</h3>
<section class="ltx_subsubsection" id="S5.SS1.SSS1">
<h4 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsubsection" data-translate-id="chunk-111">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">5.1.1 </span>Evaluation Settings</h4>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-112">Benchmarks</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-113">We evaluate Kimi K2.5 on a comprehensive benchmark suite spanning text-based reasoning, competitive and agentic coding, multimodal understanding (image and video), autonomous agentic execution, and computer use. Our benchmark taxonomy is organized along the following capability axes:</p>
<ul class="ltx_itemize" id="S5.I1">
<li class="ltx_item" id="S5.I1.i1" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S5.I1.i1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I1.i1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-114"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Reasoning &amp; General</span>: Humanity’s Last Exam (HLE)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib187" title="Humanity’s last exam">46</a>]</cite>, AIME 2025&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib243" title="2025 american invitational mathematics examination i">40</a>]</cite>, HMMT 2025 (Feb)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib244" title="Harvard-mit mathematics tournament, february 2025">58</a>]</cite>, IMO-AnswerBench&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib236" title="Towards robust mathematical reasoning">36</a>]</cite>, GPQA-Diamond&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib131" title="Gpqa: a graduate-level google-proof q&amp;a benchmark">47</a>]</cite>, MMLU-Pro&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib110" title="MMLU-pro: a more robust and challenging multi-task language understanding benchmark">64</a>]</cite>, SimpleQA Verified&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib245" title="SimpleQA verified: a reliable factuality benchmark to measure parametric knowledge">21</a>]</cite>, AdvancedIF&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib246" title="AdvancedIF: rubric-based benchmarking and reinforcement learning for advancing llm instruction following">22</a>]</cite>, and LongBench v2&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib34" title="LongBench v2: towards deeper understanding and reasoning on realistic long-context multitasks">8</a>]</cite>.</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S5.I1.i2" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S5.I1.i2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I1.i2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-115"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Coding</span>:
SWE-Bench Verified&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib238" title="Swe-bench: can language models resolve real-world github issues?">28</a>]</cite>,
SWE-Bench Pro (public)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib239" title="SWE-bench pro: can ai agents solve long-horizon software engineering tasks?">15</a>]</cite>,
SWE-Bench Multilingual&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib238" title="Swe-bench: can language models resolve real-world github issues?">28</a>]</cite>,
Terminal Bench&nbsp;2.0&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib240" title="Terminal-bench: benchmarking agents on hard, realistic tasks in command line interfaces">38</a>]</cite>,
PaperBench (CodeDev)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib193" title="PaperBench: evaluating ai’s ability to replicate ai research">52</a>]</cite>,
CyberGym&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib237" title="CyberGym: evaluating ai agents’ cybersecurity capabilities with real-world vulnerabilities at scale">66</a>]</cite>,
SciCode&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib242" title="Scicode: a research coding benchmark curated by scientists">56</a>]</cite>,
OJBench (cpp)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib241" title="OJBench: a competition level code benchmark for large language models">65</a>]</cite>,
and LiveCodeBench (v6)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib121" title="Livecodebench: holistic and contamination free evaluation of large language models for code">27</a>]</cite>.</p>

<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I1.i4.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-117"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Image Understanding</span>: <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I1.i4.p1.1.2">(math &amp; reasoning)</span> MMMU-Pro&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib205" title="MMMU-pro: a more robust multi-discipline multimodal understanding benchmark">76</a>]</cite>, MMMU (val)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib1" title="MMMU: a massive multi-discipline multimodal understanding and reasoning benchmark for expert agi">75</a>]</cite>, CharXiv (RQ)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib214" title="CharXiv: charting gaps in realistic chart understanding in multimodal llms">67</a>]</cite>, MathVision&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib206" title="Measuring multimodal mathematical reasoning with math-vision dataset">61</a>]</cite> and MathVista (mini)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib213" title="MathVista: evaluating mathematical reasoning of foundation models in visual contexts">35</a>]</cite>; <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I1.i4.p1.1.4">(perception)</span> ZeroBench (w/ and w/o tools)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib220" title="ZeroBench: an impossible visual benchmark for contemporary large multimodal models">48</a>]</cite>, BabyVision&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib207" title="BabyVision: visual reasoning beyond language">11</a>]</cite>, BLINK&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib2" title="BLINK: multimodal large language models can see but not perceive">17</a>]</cite> and MMVP&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib3" title="Eyes wide shut? exploring the visual shortcomings of multimodal llms">57</a>]</cite>; <section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-203">Baselines</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-204">We benchmark against state-of-the-art proprietary and open-source models. For proprietary models, we compare against Claude Opus 4.5 (with extended thinking) <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib226" title="Claude opus 4.5 system card">4</a>]</cite>, GPT-5.2 (with xhigh reasoning effort) <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib227" title="Introducing gpt 5.2">41</a>]</cite>, and Gemini 3 Pro (with high reasoning-level) <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib228" title="Gemini 3 pro">19</a>]</cite>. For open-source models, we include DeepSeek-V3.2 (with thinking mode enabled)&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib247" title="DeepSeek-v3.2: pushing the frontier of open large language models">13</a>]</cite> for text benchmarks, while vision benchmarks report Qwen3-VL-235B-A22B-Thinking&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib17" title="Qwen3-vl technical report">7</a>]</cite> instead.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px3">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-205">Evaluation Configurations</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS1.Px3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-206">Unless otherwise specified, all Kimi K2.5 evaluations use temperature = 1.0, top-p = 0.95, and a context length of 256k tokens. Benchmarks without publicly available scores were re-evaluated under identical conditions and marked with an asterisk (*). The full evaluation settings can be found in appendix&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#A5" title="Appendix E Evaluation Settings ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">E</span></a>.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsubsection" id="S5.SS1.SSS2">
<h4 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsubsection" data-translate-id="chunk-207">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">5.1.2 </span>Evaluation Results</h4>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-208">Comprehensive results comparing Kimi K2.5 against proprietary and open-source baselines are presented in Table&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.T4" title="Table 4 ‣ Benchmarks ‣ 5.1.1 Evaluation Settings ‣ 5.1 Main Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>. We highlight key observations across core capability domains:</p>
</div>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-209">Reasoning and General</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-210">Kimi K2.5 achieves competitive performance with top-tier proprietary models on rigorous STEM benchmarks. On Math tasks, AIME 2025, K2.5 scores 96.1%, approaching GPT-5.2’s perfect score while outperforming Claude Opus 4.5 (92.8%) and Gemini 3 Pro (95.0%). This high-level performance extends to the HMMT 2025 (95.4%) and IMO-AnswerBench (81.8%), demonstrating K2.5’s superior reasoning depth. Kimi K2.5 also exhibits remarkable knowledge and scientific reasoning capabilities, scoring 36.9% on SimpleQA Verified, 87.1% on MMLU-Pro and 87.6% on GPQA. Notably, on HLE without the use of tools, K2.5 achieves an HLE-Full score of 30.1%, with component-wise scores of 31.5% on text subset and 21.3% on image subset. When tool-use is enabled, K2.5’s HLE-Full score rises to 50.2%, with 51.8% (text) and 39.8% (image), significantly outperforming Gemini 3 Pro (45.8%) and GPT-5.2 (45.5%). In addition to reasoning and knowledge, K2.5 shows strong instruction-following performance (75.6% on AdvancedIF) and competitive long-context abilities, achieving 61.0% on LongBench v2 compared to both proprietary and open-source models.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-211">Complex Coding and Software Engineering</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-212">Kimi K2.5 exhibits strong software engineering capabilities, especially on realistic coding and maintenance tasks. It achieves 76.8% on SWE-Bench Verified and 73.0% on SWE-Bench Multilingual, outperforming Gemini 3 Pro while remaining competitive with Claude Opus 4.5 and GPT‑5.2. On LiveCodeBench v6, Kimi K2.5 reaches 85.0%, surpassing DeepSeek‑V3.2 (83.3%) and Claude Opus 4.5 (82.2%), highlighting its robustness on live, continuously updated coding challenges. On TerminalBench 2.0, PaperBench, and SciCode, it scores 50.8%, 63.5%, and 48.7% respectively, demonstrating stable competition‑level performance in automated software engineering and problem solving across diverse domains. In addition, K2.5 attains a score of 41.3 on CyberGym, on the task of finding previously discovered vulnerabilities in real open‑source software projects given only a high‑level description of the weakness, further underscoring its effectiveness in security‑oriented software analysis.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px3">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-213">Agentic Capabilities</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-214">Kimi K2.5 establishes new state-of-the-art performance on complex agentic search and browsing tasks. On BrowseComp, K2.5 achieves 60.6% without context management techniques, 74.9% with Discard-all context management <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib247" title="DeepSeek-v3.2: pushing the frontier of open large language models">13</a>]</cite> — substantially outperforming GPT-5.2’s reported 65.8%, Claude Opus 4.5 (37.0%) and Gemini 3 Pro (37.8%). Similarly, WideSearch reaches 72.7% on item-f1. On DeepSearchQA (77.1%), FinSearchCompT2&amp;T3 (67.8%) and Seal-0 (57.4%), K2.5 leads all evaluated models, demonstrating superior capacity for agentic deep research, information synthesis, and multi-step tool orchestration.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px4">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-215">Vision Reasoning, Knowledge and Perception</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px4.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px4.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-216">Kimi K2.5 demonstrates strong visual reasoning and world knowledge capabilities. It scores 78.5% on MMMU-Pro, spanning multi-disciplinary multimodal tasks. For world knowledge question answering, K2.5 achieves 71.2% on SimpleVQA and 46.3% on WorldVQA. For visual reasoning, it achieves 84.2% on MathVision, 90.1% on MathVista (mini), and 36.5% on BabyVision. For OCR and document understanding, K2.5 delivers outstanding results with 77.5% on CharXiv (RQ), 92.3% on OCRBench, 88.8% on OmniDocBench 1.5, and 92.6% on InfoVQA (test). On the challenging ZeroBench, Kimi K2.5 achieves 9% and 11% with tool augmentation, substantially ahead of competing models. On basic visual perception benchmarks BLINK (78.9%) and MMVP (87.0%), we also observe competitive performance of Kimi K2.5, demonstrating its robust real-world visual perceptions.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px5">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-217">Video Understanding</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px5.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px5.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-218">Kimi K2.5 achieves state-of-the-art performance across diverse video understanding tasks. It attains 86.6% on VideoMMMU and 80.4% on MMVU, rivaling frontier leaderships. With the context-compression and dense temporal understanding abilities of MoonViT-3D, Kimi K2.5 also establishes new global SOTA records in long-video comprehension with 75.9% on LVBench and 79.8% on LongVideoBench by feeding over 2,000 frames, while demonstrating robust dense-motion understanding at 70.4% on the highly-dimensional MotionBench.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px6">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-219">Computer-Use Capability</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px6.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS1.SSS2.Px6.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-220">Kimi K2.5 demonstrates state-of-the-art computer-use capability on real-world tasks. On the computer-use benchmark OSWorld-Verified&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib5" title="Introducing osworld-verified">72</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib4" title="OSWorld: benchmarking multimodal agents for open-ended tasks in real computer environments">73</a>]</cite>, it achieves a 63.3% success rate relying solely on GUI actions without external tools. This substantially outperforms open-source models such as Qwen3-VL-235B-A22B (38.1%) and OpenAI’s computer-use agent framework Operator (o3-based) (42.9%), while remaining competitive with the current leading CUA model, Claude Opus 4.5 (66.3%). On WebArena&nbsp;<cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib250" title="WebArena: a realistic web environment for building autonomous agents">80</a>]</cite>, an established benchmark for GUI-based web browsing, Kimi K2.5 achieves a 58.9% success rate, surpassing OpenAI’s Operator (58.1%) and approaching the performance of Claude Opus 4.5 (63.4%).</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S5.SS2">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection" data-translate-id="chunk-221">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.2 </span>Agent Swarm Results</h3>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px1">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-222">Benchmarks</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-223">To rigorously evaluate the effectiveness of the agent swarm framework, we select three representative benchmarks that collectively cover deep reasoning, large-scale retrieval, and real-world complexity:</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p2">
<ul class="ltx_itemize" id="S5.I2">
<li class="ltx_item" id="S5.I2.i1" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.I2.i1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I2.i1.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-224"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i1.p1.1.1">BrowseComp</span>: A challenging deep-research benchmark that requires multi-step reasoning and complex information synthesis.</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S5.I2.i2" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.I2.i2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I2.i2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-225"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i2.p1.1.1">WideSearch</span>: A benchmark designed to evaluate the ability to perform broad, multi-step information seeking and reasoning across diverse sources.</p>
</div>
</li>
<li class="ltx_item" id="S5.I2.i3" style="list-style-type:none;">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S5.I2.i3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-226"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.1">In-house Swarm Bench</span>: An internally developed Swarm benchmark, designed to evaluate the agent swarm performance under real-world, high-complexity conditions. It covers four domains: <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.2">WildSearch</span> (unconstrained, real-world information retrieval over the open web), <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.3">Batch Download</span> (large-scale acquisition of diverse resources), <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.4">WideRead</span> (large-scale document comprehension involving more than 100 input documents), and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.5">Long-Form Writing</span> (coherent generation of extensive content exceeding 100k words). This benchmark incorporates extreme-scale scenarios that stress-test the orchestration, scalability, and coordination capabilities of agent-based systems.</p>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_table" id="S5.T6">

</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px2">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-237">Performance</h5>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-238">Table&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.T6" title="Table 6 ‣ Benchmarks ‣ 5.2 Agent Swarm Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">6</span></a> presents the performance of Kimi K2.5 Agent Swarm against single-agent configurations and proprietary baselines. The results demonstrate substantial performance improvements from multi-agent orchestration. On BrowseComp, Agent Swarm achieves 78.4%, representing a 17.8% absolute gain over the single-agent K2.5 (60.6%) and surpassing even GPT-5.2 Pro (77.9%). Similarly, WideSearch sees a 6.3% improvement (72.7% <math alttext="\to" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo stretchy="false">→</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\to</annotation></semantics></math> 79.0%) on Item-F1, enabling K2.5 Agent Swarm to outperform Claude Opus 4.5 (76.2%) and establish a new state-of-the-art. The gains are most pronounced on In-house Swarm bench (16.7%), where tasks are explicitly designed to reward parallel decomposition. These consistent improvements across benchmarks validate that Agent Swarm effectively converts computational parallelism into qualitative capability gains, particularly for problems requiring broad exploration, multi-source verification, or simultaneous handling of independent sub-tasks.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S5.F7">
<div class="ltx_flex_figure">
<div class="ltx_flex_cell ltx_flex_size_2">

<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-240"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 7: </span>Comparison of Kimi K2.5 performance under Agent Swarm and Discard-all context management in BrowseComp.</figcaption>

</div>
</div>
</figure>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S5.F8"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="296" id="S5.F8.g1" src="x6.png" width="513">
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering" data-translate-id="chunk-241"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 8: </span>Agent Swarm achieves 3<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.F8.3.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math>–4.5<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.F8.4.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> faster execution time compared to single-agent baselines as target Item-F1 increases from 30% to 70% in WideSearch testing.</figcaption>
</figure>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-242">Execution Time Savings via Parallelism</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.5" data-translate-id="chunk-243">Beyond improved task performance, Agent Swarm achieves substantial wall-clock time reductions through parallel subagent execution. On the WideSearch benchmark, it reduces the execution time required to reach target performance by 3<math alttext="\times\sim" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.m1" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mo>×</mo><mo lspace="0em">⁣</mo><mo>∼</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times\sim</annotation></semantics></math> 4.5<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.2.m2" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> compared to a single-agent baseline. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.F8" title="Figure 8 ‣ Performance ‣ 5.2 Agent Swarm Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">8</span></a>, this efficiency gain scales with task complexity: as the target Item-F1 increases from 30% to 70%, the single agent’s execution time grows from approximately 1.8<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.3.m3" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> to over 7.0<math alttext="\times" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.4.m4" intent=":literal"><semantics><mo>×</mo><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math> the baseline, whereas Agent Swarm maintains near-constant low latency in the range of <math alttext="0.6\times\sim 1.6\times" class="ltx_math_unparsed" display="inline" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px3.p1.5.m5" intent=":literal"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.6</mn><mo lspace="0.222em" rspace="0em">×</mo><mo lspace="0em">∼</mo><mn>1.6</mn><mo lspace="0.222em">×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.6\times\sim 1.6\times</annotation></semantics></math>. These results indicate that Agent Swarm effectively transforms sequential tool invocations into parallel operations, preventing the linear growth in completion time typically observed as task difficulty increases.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px4">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-244">Dynamic Subagent Creation and Scheduling</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px4.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px4.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-245">Within an agent swarm, subagents are dynamically instantiated rather than
pre-defined. Through PARL, the orchestrator learns adaptive policies to create and
schedule self-hosted subagents in response to evolving task structures
and problem states. Unlike static decomposition approaches, this learned policy enables the Orchestrator to reason about the requisite number, timing, and specialization of subagents based on query. Consequently, a heterogeneous agent group emerges organically from this adaptive allocation strategy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.F7" title="Figure 7 ‣ Performance ‣ 5.2 Agent Swarm Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">7</span></a>).</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_paragraph" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5">
<h5 class="ltx_title ltx_title_paragraph" data-translate-id="chunk-246">Agent Swarm as Proactive Context Management</h5>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-247">Beyond better performance and runtime acceleration, an agent swarm is a kind of proactive and intelligent context management enabled by multi-agent architecture <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib223" title="Building multi-agent systems: when and how to use them">6</a>]</cite>. This approach differs from test-time context truncation strategies such as Hide-Tool-Result <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib224" title="Kimi-researcher end-to-end rl training for emerging agentic capabilities">2</a>]</cite>, Summary <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib248" title="ReSum: unlocking long-horizon search intelligence via context summarization">71</a>]</cite>, or Discard-all <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#bib.bib247" title="DeepSeek-v3.2: pushing the frontier of open large language models">13</a>]</cite>, which react to context overflow by compressing or discarding accumulated histories. While effective at reducing token usage, these methods are inherently reactive and often sacrifice structural information or intermediate reasoning.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p2.1" data-translate-id="chunk-248">In contrast, Agent Swarm enables proactive context control through explicit orchestration. Long-horizon tasks are decomposed into parallel, semantically isolated subtasks, each executed by a specialized subagent with a bounded local context. Crucially, these subagents maintain independent working memories and perform local reasoning without directly mutating or contaminating the global context of the central orchestrator. Only task-relevant outputs—rather than full interaction traces—are selectively routed back to the orchestrator. This design induces context sharding rather than context truncation, allowing the system to scale effective context length along an additional architectural dimension while preserving modularity, information locality, and reasoning integrity.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S5.SS2.SSS0.Px5.p3.1" data-translate-id="chunk-249">As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2602.02276v1#S5.F7" title="Figure 7 ‣ Performance ‣ 5.2 Agent Swarm Results ‣ 5 Evaluations ‣ Kimi K2.5: Visual Agentic Intelligence"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">7</span></a>, this proactive strategy outperforms Discard-all in both efficiency and accuracy on BrowseComp. By preserving task-level coherence at the orchestrator level while keeping subagent contexts tightly bounded, Agent Swarm enables parallel execution with selective context persistence, retaining only high-level coordination signals or essential intermediate results. Consequently, Agent Swarm operates as an active, structured context manager, achieving higher accuracy with substantially fewer critical steps than uniform context truncation.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S6">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section" data-translate-id="chunk-250">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">6 </span>Conclusions</h2>
<div class="ltx_para ltx_noindent" id="S6.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S6.p1.1" data-translate-id="chunk-251">Kimi K2.5 shows that scalable and general agentic intelligence can be achieved through joint optimization of text and vision together with parallel agent execution. By unifying language and vision across pre-training and reinforcement learning, the model achieves strong cross-modal alignment and visual–text reasoning. Agent Swarm enables concurrent execution of heterogeneous sub-tasks, reducing inference latency while improving performance on complex agentic workloads. Grounded in vision–text intelligence and agent swarms, Kimi K2.5 demonstrates strong performance on benchmarks and real-world tasks. By open-sourcing the post-trained checkpoints, we aim to support the open-source community in building scalable and general-purpose agentic systems and to accelerate progress toward General Agentic Intelligence.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_pagination ltx_role_newpage"></div>
</section>

<li class="ltx_bibitem ltx_bib_misc" id="bib.bib9">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_bib_key ltx_role_refnum ltx_tag_bibitem">[10]</span>
<span class="ltx_bibblock"><span class="ltx_text ltx_bib_author">T. B. Brown, B. Mann, N. Ryder, M. Subbiah, J. Kaplan, P. Dhariwal, A. Neelakantan, P. Shyam, G. Sastry, A. Askell, S. Agarwal, A.
